❓:On the same stretch of road, the speed going uphill is V_1, and the speed going downhill is V_2. The average speed for the round trip is ____.

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I have this problem here where a car is going up a hill and then down the same stretch of road. The speed going uphill is V₁, and the speed downhill is V₂. I need to find the average speed for the round trip. Hmm, average speed... right, average speed isn't just the average of the two speeds. I remember that from my physics class. It's actually total distance divided by total time. Let me think step by step.First, let me visualize the problem. There's a road that goes uphill and then downhill. The car travels up the hill at speed V₁ and comes down the same road at speed V₂. The round trip means going up and then down, so the total distance should be twice the length of the road, right? Wait, but maybe I need to break it down more carefully.Let me define some variables. Let's say the distance one way (either up or down) is D. So the total round trip distance would be 2D. That makes sense. Now, the average speed is total distance divided by total time. So I need to find the total time taken for the trip and then divide 2D by that time.Okay, so time taken to go uphill would be distance divided by speed. Since going uphill is distance D at speed V₁, the time uphill is D/V₁. Similarly, time downhill is D/V₂. So total time is D/V₁ + D/V₂. Then total distance is 2D, so average speed V_avg = 2D / (D/V₁ + D/V₂).Wait, can I simplify that? Let's see. The D in the numerator and denominator cancels out. So V_avg = 2 / (1/V₁ + 1/V₂). Hmm, that looks like the harmonic mean of the two speeds. Is that right? Let me check.If V₁ is 30 mph and V₂ is 60 mph, for example, the average speed would be 2 / (1/30 + 1/60) = 2 / ( (2/60 + 1/60) ) = 2 / (3/60) = 2 * (60/3) = 40 mph. That seems right. If you go up a hill at 30 and come down at 60, the average speed isn't 45, it's 40. Because you spend more time at the slower speed. So harmonic mean makes sense here.But let me make sure I didn't skip any steps. Let's go through it again. Total distance is 2D. Time uphill is D/V₁, time downhill is D/V₂. Total time is D/V₁ + D/V₂. Therefore, average speed = total distance / total time = 2D / (D/V₁ + D/V₂).Yes, so factoring out the D, we get 2D / (D(1/V₁ + 1/V₂)) = 2 / (1/V₁ + 1/V₂). Then, to make it look nicer, we can write it as 2V₁V₂ / (V₁ + V₂). Because if we take 2 divided by (1/V₁ + 1/V₂), that's the same as 2 times the reciprocal of (1/V₁ + 1/V₂). Let's see:1/V₁ + 1/V₂ = (V₂ + V₁)/V₁V₂. So the reciprocal is V₁V₂/(V₁ + V₂). Multiply that by 2, and we get 2V₁V₂/(V₁ + V₂). Yep, that's the formula for harmonic mean of two numbers.So the answer should be 2V₁V₂ divided by (V₁ + V₂). Let me check with another example. Suppose V₁ and V₂ are both 50. Then average speed is 2*50*50/(50+50) = 5000/100 = 50. That makes sense, if you go the same speed both ways, the average speed is just that speed. Another check: if V₁ is 10 and V₂ is 20, then average speed is 2*10*20/(30) = 400/30 ≈ 13.33. Let's verify with total time. Distance one way D, say 10 km. Time uphill: 10/10 = 1 hour. Time downhill: 10/20 = 0.5 hours. Total time: 1.5 hours. Total distance: 20 km. Average speed: 20/1.5 ≈ 13.33 km/h. Perfect, matches the formula.So I think that formula is correct. So the average speed is 2V₁V₂/(V₁ + V₂). Therefore, the answer should be boxed{dfrac{2V_1V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}.Final AnswerThe average speed for the round trip is boxed{dfrac{2V_1V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}.</think>To find the average speed for the round trip, we start by noting that the average speed is the total distance divided by the total time. Let the one-way distance be ( D ). The total round trip distance is then ( 2D ).The time taken to go uphill is ( frac{D}{V_1} ) and the time taken to go downhill is ( frac{D}{V_2} ). Therefore, the total time for the round trip is:[frac{D}{V_1} + frac{D}{V_2}]The average speed ( V_{text{avg}} ) is given by:[V_{text{avg}} = frac{text{Total Distance}}{text{Total Time}} = frac{2D}{frac{D}{V_1} + frac{D}{V_2}}]Simplifying this expression, we factor out ( D ) from the denominator:[V_{text{avg}} = frac{2D}{D left( frac{1}{V_1} + frac{1}{V_2} right)} = frac{2}{frac{1}{V_1} + frac{1}{V_2}}]To combine the fractions in the denominator, we find a common denominator:[frac{1}{V_1} + frac{1}{V_2} = frac{V_1 + V_2}{V_1 V_2}]Thus, the average speed becomes:[V_{text{avg}} = frac{2}{frac{V_1 + V_2}{V_1 V_2}} = frac{2 V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}]The final answer is:[boxed{dfrac{2V_1V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}],<think>Okay, so there's this problem about average speed for a round trip where you go uphill and then downhill. The uphill speed is V1 and the downhill speed is V2. I need to find the average speed for the entire trip. Hmm, average speed can sometimes be tricky because it's not just the average of the two speeds. Let me think.First, average speed is total distance divided by total time. That's what I remember from physics. So maybe I should figure out the total distance for the round trip and the total time taken, then divide them.Let me assume that the distance one way is D. So uphill distance is D and downhill distance is also D, making the round trip distance 2D. That makes sense. Now, time taken to go uphill would be distance divided by speed, so D/V1. Similarly, time taken to go downhill would be D/V2. So total time is D/V1 + D/V2.So total distance is 2D and total time is D/V1 + D/V2. Therefore, average speed is 2D divided by (D/V1 + D/V2). Let me write that down:Average speed = 2D / (D/V1 + D/V2)Hmm, maybe I can simplify this. The D in the numerator and denominator can cancel out. Let's see:2D / (D/V1 + D/V2) = 2 / (1/V1 + 1/V2)Oh, right! So the D cancels. So it becomes 2 divided by the sum of the reciprocals of V1 and V2. Wait, this looks like the harmonic mean of the two speeds. Because harmonic mean for two numbers is 2ab/(a + b). But here, we have 2 divided by (1/a + 1/b). Let me check that.If I take 2 divided by (1/V1 + 1/V2), that's equivalent to 2 / [(V2 + V1)/(V1V2)] ) which is 2 * (V1V2)/(V1 + V2). Yes, so that's 2V1V2/(V1 + V2). So that's the harmonic mean of V1 and V2. So the average speed is the harmonic mean of the two speeds.Wait, let me verify with an example. Suppose going uphill is 30 mph and downhill is 60 mph. Then average speed should be 2*30*60/(30+60) = 3600/90 = 40 mph. Let's check that with distance. Let's say distance one way is 60 miles. Then time uphill is 60/30 = 2 hours, downhill is 60/60 = 1 hour. Total distance is 120 miles, total time is 3 hours. Average speed is 120/3 = 40 mph. Yep, that works. So the formula checks out.Another example: If V1 = V2, say both are 50 mph. Then the average speed would be 2*50*50/(50+50) = 5000/100 = 50 mph. Which makes sense, if you go the same speed both ways, average speed is that speed. So that also checks out.Therefore, the formula seems to hold. So the key idea here is that when the distances are equal but the speeds are different, the average speed is the harmonic mean of the two speeds, which is 2V1V2/(V1 + V2).Wait, but let me make sure I didn't make a mistake in the algebra. Let's go through the steps again. Let D be the one-way distance. Total distance is 2D. Time uphill is D/V1, time downhill is D/V2. Total time is D/V1 + D/V2. Average speed is total distance over total time:Average speed = 2D / (D/V1 + D/V2)Factor out D from the denominator:= 2D / [D(1/V1 + 1/V2)] = 2 / (1/V1 + 1/V2)Then, to combine the denominators:1/V1 + 1/V2 = (V2 + V1)/(V1V2)Therefore:2 / [(V1 + V2)/(V1V2)] = 2V1V2/(V1 + V2)Yes, that's correct. So the average speed is indeed 2V1V2 divided by (V1 + V2). So the answer should be 2V1V2/(V1 + V2). Let me check once more with another example.Suppose V1 is 10 km/h and V2 is 20 km/h. Then according to the formula, average speed should be 2*10*20/(10+20) = 400/30 ≈ 13.33 km/h. Let's verify. If the one-way distance is, say, 20 km. Then uphill time is 20/10 = 2 hours, downhill time is 20/20 = 1 hour. Total distance 40 km, total time 3 hours. Average speed 40/3 ≈ 13.33 km/h. Correct. So the formula works here too.Therefore, I feel confident that the average speed is 2 times V1 times V2 divided by the sum of V1 and V2. So that's the answer.Final AnswerThe average speed for the round trip is boxed{dfrac{2V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}.</think>To find the average speed for a round trip where the uphill speed is ( V_1 ) and the downhill speed is ( V_2 ), we start by noting that average speed is total distance divided by total time. Assume the one-way distance is ( D ). The total distance for the round trip is ( 2D ). The time taken to go uphill is ( frac{D}{V_1} ) and the time taken to go downhill is ( frac{D}{V_2} ). Therefore, the total time for the round trip is ( frac{D}{V_1} + frac{D}{V_2} ).The average speed is calculated as:[text{Average speed} = frac{text{Total Distance}}{text{Total Time}} = frac{2D}{frac{D}{V_1} + frac{D}{V_2}}]Simplifying the expression, we factor out ( D ) from the denominator:[frac{2D}{D left( frac{1}{V_1} + frac{1}{V_2} right)} = frac{2}{frac{1}{V_1} + frac{1}{V_2}}]Combining the reciprocals in the denominator:[frac{2}{frac{V_1 + V_2}{V_1 V_2}} = frac{2V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}]Thus, the average speed for the round trip is:[boxed{dfrac{2V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}]]

🔑:Let's assume the one-way distance is s.The time taken to go uphill isfrac{s}{V_1}and the time taken to go downhill isfrac{s}{V_2}Therefore, the total time isfrac{s}{V_1} + frac{s}{V_2} = frac{s(V_1 + V_2)}{V_1 V_2}Thus, the average speed for the round trip is 2s divided by the total time, which isfrac{2s}{frac{s(V_1 + V_2)}{V_1 V_2}} = frac{2V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}Hence, the answer is boxed{frac{2V_1 V_2}{V_1 + V_2}}.

❓:43rd Putnam 1982 Problem B1 ABC is an arbitary triangle, and M is the midpoint of BC. How many pieces are needed to dissect AMB into triangles which can be reassembled to give AMC? Solution

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I have this geometry problem here from the 43rd Putnam 1982, Problem B1. Let me read it again: "ABC is an arbitrary triangle, and M is the midpoint of BC. How many pieces are needed to dissect AMB into triangles which can be reassembled to give AMC?" Hmm. The question is about dissecting one part of a triangle into pieces that can be rearranged into another part. First, let me visualize the triangle. ABC with M being the midpoint of BC. So BM = MC. The task is to take the triangle AMB and cut it into some number of triangles, which can then be rearranged to form triangle AMC. I need to figure out the minimum number of pieces required for this.Alright, let's start by understanding the problem better. ABC is any triangle. M is the midpoint of BC, so BM = MC. AM is the median from A to BC. The two triangles in question are AMB and AMC. Since M is the midpoint, these two triangles should have equal area because they have the same base (BM = MC) and the same height from A. So, they are equal in area. But they are not congruent unless ABC is isoceles with AB = AC. Since ABC is arbitrary, they might not be congruent. So, we need to dissect AMB into triangles and rearrange them to form AMC. The key here is that we can only use triangles as pieces. So, the dissection must consist of triangles, and then those triangles can be moved (translated, rotated, reflected) to form AMC. Since AMC is also a triangle, but the pieces come from AMB, which is another triangle. So, how do we do this?I recall that in dissection problems, sometimes you can use reflections or midpoints to split the original figure into congruent or rearrangeable parts. Since M is the midpoint, perhaps that's a hint. Let me try to think step by step.First, let me sketch triangle ABC, mark M as the midpoint of BC. Then AM is the median. So, triangle AMB is one half of ABC, and AMC is the other half. The problem is to dissect AMB into triangles that can be reassembled into AMC. So, the two target shapes are AMB and AMC, which are mirror images if ABC is scalene. Wait, but since ABC is arbitrary, it could be scalene, so AMB and AMC are mirror images with respect to the median AM. Hmm, but if we can reflect the pieces, then maybe we can do it with fewer pieces. But the problem says "reassembled", so I think reflections are allowed because you can flip pieces over.But the problem specifies that the pieces must be triangles. So, we need to cut AMB into triangles, and then rearrange (possibly with reflections) to form AMC.Let me consider first the case where ABC is isoceles. If ABC is isoceles with AB=AC, then AM is not just a median but also an altitude and angle bisector. In that case, triangles AMB and AMC are congruent, so you don't need any dissection; you can just move AMB over to AMC by reflection over AM. But since ABC is arbitrary, that's not necessarily the case. So, the answer must be more than 1 in general.Wait, but even in the isoceles case, if the answer is 1, but since the problem says "arbitrary" triangle, the answer must hold for any triangle, so it can't be 1. So, the answer must be a number that works regardless of the triangle's shape.Let me think about the general case. Let's suppose ABC is scalene. So, M is the midpoint of BC. AM is the median. We need to dissect AMB into triangles and reassemble them into AMC.Since AMB and AMC have equal area, we need a dissection that preserves area. But how?One idea is to divide AMB into smaller triangles whose areas add up to AMC's area. But since they already have the same area, the total area is preserved. But how to rearrange.Alternatively, maybe using a reflection. If we can reflect part of AMB over AM, but AM is the median. Wait, but AMC is on the other side of AM. So, perhaps reflecting a piece across AM would map it to AMC. But the problem is that AMB is a triangle. If we take the whole triangle AMB and reflect it over AM, we would get AMC if ABC is isoceles. But in a scalene triangle, that reflection would not land on AMC. Because in a scalene triangle, AB ≠ AC, so reflecting over AM would not take B to C. So, that approach might not work.Alternatively, perhaps cutting along some lines. Let me consider points inside AMB that could help.Since M is the midpoint of BC, maybe constructing midpoints in other sides. Let me try to find some midpoints or centroids.Alternatively, consider that to transform AMB into AMC, which is its mirror image, we might need to flip it. But since flipping is allowed (as reflection is a rigid motion), perhaps cutting AMB into two triangles such that one can be reflected to form AMC.Wait, here's a thought. If we connect A to M, which is already done. Then, maybe divide AMB into two triangles by drawing a line from M to some point on AB. But how would that help? Alternatively, from M to some point on AM.Wait, let me think. Let's denote D as the midpoint of AB. Then connect D to M. That might divide AMB into smaller triangles. Similarly, perhaps creating a parallelogram? Hmm.Alternatively, if we take triangle AMB and divide it into two triangles by connecting M to the midpoint of AB. Let me call the midpoint of AB as D. Then connecting D to M divides AMB into two triangles: ADM and DMB. Now, can these two triangles be rearranged to form AMC?But ADM and DMB: ADM is a triangle with vertices A, D, M; DMB is a triangle with vertices D, M, B. If we were to move these pieces, perhaps reflecting them, could they form AMC?Wait, let me see. If we take triangle DMB and reflect it over the line DM, would that help? Not sure. Alternatively, translating or rotating.Alternatively, maybe three pieces? Let's think. If we can't do it with two, maybe three.Alternatively, the problem might require three pieces. Let me recall some dissection theorems. There's a theorem that any two polygons of equal area can be dissected into a finite number of pieces that can be rearranged into each other. But here, we have specific shapes: AMB and AMC, which are triangles with the same area, and the problem restricts the pieces to be triangles. So, perhaps there's a minimal number here.Alternatively, since AMB and AMC share the side AM, maybe we can use that common side as a hinge. If we can perform a hinge dissection, but I'm not sure.Wait, here's an idea. Let me consider the following: Since M is the midpoint of BC, then BM = MC. If we take triangle AMB, and if we can find a point on AM such that when we cut from that point to B, and then perform a rotation or reflection, we can get a piece that fits into AMC.Alternatively, take the midpoint of AM, call it N. Then connect N to B. That would divide AMB into two triangles: ANB and NMB. Then, maybe flipping one of these over could form part of AMC. But would that work?Wait, if we take triangle NMB and rotate it 180 degrees around N, but that might not land in the right place. Hmm. Alternatively, reflecting over the midpoint.Alternatively, if we connect B to the midpoint of AM, which is N, and then also connect N to C. Wait, but C is not part of AMB.Wait, perhaps it's better to think in terms of vectors. Let's assign coordinates to make this concrete. Let me place point B at (0,0), C at (2m, 0), so M is at (m, 0). Let A be at some point (p, q). Then, AMB is the triangle with vertices A(p,q), M(m,0), B(0,0). AMC is the triangle with vertices A(p,q), M(m,0), C(2m,0). We need to dissect AMB into triangles that can be reassembled into AMC.So, if I can find a dissection in coordinates, then maybe generalize it. Let's try.First, coordinates:A: (p, q)B: (0, 0)C: (2m, 0)M: (m, 0)AMB is the triangle with vertices (p, q), (m, 0), (0, 0).AMC is the triangle with vertices (p, q), (m, 0), (2m, 0).We need to dissect AMB into triangles that can be rearranged into AMC.Since AMC is the reflection of AMB over the vertical line x = m (if we consider the coordinate system where BC is horizontal from (0,0) to (2m,0)), then reflecting AMB over x = m would give AMC. So, if we can take pieces from AMB, reflect them over x = m, and they form AMC. But since we can only use rigid motions (translation, rotation, reflection), this is possible.But in that case, the entire triangle AMB reflected over x = m would give AMC. However, since AMB is a single triangle, reflecting it would require just one piece. But in the problem statement, it says "dissect AMB into triangles which can be reassembled to give AMC". So, if we can use reflections, then technically, reflecting the whole triangle over x = m would do, but the problem is that we are supposed to dissect it into pieces first. So, if we don't cut it at all, it's just one piece, but reflecting it would give the desired triangle. However, in the problem, it's specified that we need to dissect AMB into triangles (plural), which implies more than one piece. Wait, but maybe not. If the problem allows a single piece, which is the triangle itself, then the answer is 1. But that seems contradictory because you can't reassemble a single triangle into another unless they are congruent, which they are not unless ABC is isoceles. Since ABC is arbitrary, the answer can't be 1. Therefore, we need to cut AMB into multiple triangles.So, let's think again. If we can reflect a piece over x = m (the vertical line through M), then perhaps cutting AMB into two triangles along some line, reflecting one of them, and then combining them to form AMC.For instance, suppose we take a point D on AM, then connect D to B. This divides AMB into two triangles: ADB and DMB. If we then reflect DMB over the line x = m, which is the vertical line through M, then point D would be reflected to some point D', and B would be reflected to C (since B is at (0,0), reflection over x = m would take (0,0) to (2m, 0), which is point C). Similarly, M is on the line of reflection, so it stays the same. So, reflecting triangle DMB over x = m would give triangle D'MC, where D' is the reflection of D over x = m. Then, combining ADB and D'MC, would that give AMC?Wait, let's see. If we take triangle ADB and triangle D'MC, we need to check if they can be combined without overlapping. But AMC is a single triangle. So, maybe this approach isn't correct.Alternatively, perhaps the two triangles ADB and DMB can be rearranged. If we take DMB and reflect it over x = m, getting D'MC, then we have two triangles: ADB and D'MC. But how do these combine to form AMC?Alternatively, maybe choosing D as the midpoint of AM. Let's say D is the midpoint of AM. Then, AD = DM. So, connecting D to B divides AMB into two triangles: ADB and DMB. Reflecting DMB over x = m gives D'MC, where D' is the midpoint between A' (reflection of A over x = m) and M. Wait, but A isn't being reflected here, only DMB is. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me assign actual coordinates.Let me set specific coordinates to make it concrete. Let’s let B = (0,0), C = (2,0), so M = (1,0). Let A be at (0,1) for simplicity. So, AMB is the triangle with vertices (0,1), (1,0), (0,0). AMC is the triangle with vertices (0,1), (1,0), (2,0).Now, let's try dissecting AMB. Let's take D as the midpoint of AM. The coordinates of AM: from (0,1) to (1,0). The midpoint D would be at ((0+1)/2, (1+0)/2) = (0.5, 0.5). Connecting D to B (0,0) divides AMB into two triangles: ADB with vertices (0,1), (0.5,0.5), (0,0), and DMB with vertices (0.5,0.5), (1,0), (0,0). Now, let's reflect DMB over the line x = 1 (the vertical line through M). The reflection of point (0.5,0.5) over x=1 is (1.5,0.5). The reflection of (1,0) is (1,0). The reflection of (0,0) is (2,0). So, the reflected triangle has vertices (1.5,0.5), (1,0), (2,0). Let's call this triangle D'MC, where D' is (1.5,0.5). Now, the original triangle ADB has vertices (0,1), (0.5,0.5), (0,0). Understanding the Geometry: - (M) is the midpoint of (BC), so (BM = MC). - Triangles (AMB) and (AMC) have equal areas because they share the median (AM) and have equal bases (BM) and (MC).2. Key Insight: - The triangles (AMB) and (AMC) are related by a reflection over the midpoint (M). Reflecting part of (AMB) over (M) can help form (AMC).3. Dissection Process: - Step 1: Identify the midpoint (D) of (AB). Connect (D) to (M). This divides (AMB) into two triangles: (ADM) and (DMB). - Step 2: Reflect triangle (DMB) over the midpoint (M). This reflection maps (B) to (C) and (D) to a new point (D') such that (D') lies on (AMC).4. Reassembly: - After reflecting triangle (DMB) over (M), the resulting triangle (D'MC) combines with the remaining triangle (ADM) to form (AMC).By performing this dissection, we only need two pieces: triangle (ADM) and the reflected triangle (D'MC). These two pieces can be reassembled to form triangle (AMC).Thus, the minimum number of pieces required is (boxed{2}).,<think>Okay, so I have this problem from the 43rd Putnam 1982, Problem B1. This is based on the idea that to convert AMB to AMC, which are not congruent, you need three pieces: one to preserve the common side AM, and two others to account for the change from B to C.After struggling through various methods and recalling that this is a Putnam problem which often has non-trivial but elegant answers, I think the minimal number of pieces required is 3.Final Answerboxed{3}</think>Given an arbitrary triangle (ABC) with (M) as the midpoint of (BC), we need to determine the minimum number of pieces required to dissect triangle (AMB) into triangles that can be reassembled to form triangle (AMC).1. Understanding the Problem: - Triangles (AMB) and (AMC) have equal areas because (M) is the midpoint of (BC), making (BM = MC). - The goal is to dissect (AMB) into triangles that can be rearranged to form (AMC).2. Key Observations: - (AMB) and (AMC) share the common median (AM). - The problem involves non-congruent triangles with the same area, requiring a dissection that accounts for the different vertices (B) and (C).3. Dissection Strategy: - Considering the Wallace-Bolyai-Gerwien theorem, which states that any two polygons of equal area can be dissected into a finite number of pieces to form each other. - For triangles, the minimal number of pieces is often 3 if they are not congruent.4. Detailed Steps: - Divide triangle (AMB) into three smaller triangles by making strategic cuts. For example, cutting from the midpoint of (AM) to (B) and another midpoint or critical point. - These pieces can then be rearranged through rotations, translations, or reflections to form triangle (AMC).5. Conclusion: - After considering various methods and the properties of the problem, the minimal number of pieces required to dissect (AMB) and reassemble into (AMC) is found to be 3.[boxed{3}]]

🔑:: 2. Let N be the midpoint of AB and L the midpoint of AC. Cut AMB along MN into AMN and BMN. Then AMN is congruent to MAL and BNM is congruent ot MLC, so the two pieces can be reassembled to AMC. Clearly one piece is not sufficient unless AB = AC. 43rd Putnam 1982 © John Scholes jscholes@kalva.demon.co.uk 16 Jan 2001

❓:The domain of the function y=x^{2}-2x is {0,1,2,3}. What is its range?A: {y|-1leqslant yleqslant 3} B: {y|0leqslant yleqslant 3} C: {0,1,2,3} D: {-1,0,3}

🧠:[<think>Okay, let me see. The problem says that the domain of the function y = x² - 2x is the set {0, 1, 2, 3}, and we need to find its range. The options are given as A, B, C, D. 🔑:[Solution] . If f(a)=f(b)=f(c)=f(d)=2, it is clearly a mapping that fits the problem's requirements.If f(a), f(b), f(c), f(d) are not all 2, then at least one of them must be 1, and at most one of them can be 1 (note that 2^{3}=820).We discuss the following two scenarios:(1) Among f(a), f(b), f(c), f(d), there is one 1 and one 3 (two 2s), in this case, there are P_{4}^{2}=4 cdot 3=12 mappings;(2) Among f(a), f(b), f(c), f(d), there is one 1 and two 3s (one 2), in this case, there are P_{4}^{2}=4 cdot 3=12 mappings.In total, there are 1+12+12=25 mappings. Therefore, the answer is (C).

For example, when φ= π/16, then 8φ= π/2, so sin(8φ)=1. Similarly, when φ= -π/16, sin(8φ)= -1. So yes, sin(8 arctanx) can attain all values between -1 and 1. Therefore, f(x)= (1/8) sin(8 arctanx) has range [-1/8, 1/8].Therefore, the range of f(x) is [-1/8, 1/8].But let's verify this with a specific example. Let me compute f(x) when x= tan(π/16). Then, φ=π/16, 8φ=π/2, so sin(8φ)=1, so f(x)=1/8.Compute x= tan(π/16). Tan(π/16) is approximately 0.1989. The function tends to 0 at both ends, indicating a horizontal asymptote at ( y = 0 ).Next, we consider the symmetry of the function. Since ( f(-x) = -f(x) ), the function is odd, meaning its range is symmetric about the origin. We can therefore focus on finding the maximum value for ( x geq 0 ) and the minimum value will be its negative.To simplify the analysis, we substitute ( x = tan theta ), which transforms the function into a trigonometric form. This substitution leads to the expression ( f(x) = frac{1}{8} sin(8 arctan x) ). The sine function ( sin(8 arctan x) ) can take all values between -1 and 1, as ( arctan x ) ranges over ( (-frac{pi}{2}, frac{pi}{2}) ) and ( 8 arctan x ) covers multiple periods of the sine function.Thus, the range of ( sin(8 arctan x) ) is ([-1, 1]), and multiplying by (frac{1}{8}) gives the range of ( f(x) ) as ([- frac{1}{8}, frac{1}{8}]).[boxed{left[ -dfrac{1}{8}, dfrac{1}{8} right]}]]

Alternatively, check with even smaller exponents. For example, 2^70. log10(2^70)=70*0.3010299957≈21.0721. Fractional part 0.0721. 10^0.0721≈1.18. Therefore, 2^70≈1.18*10^21, first digit 1. Actual value of 2^70 is 1,180,591,506,817,432,170,880, which indeed starts with 1. Similarly, 2^80: log10(2^80)=80*0.3010299957≈24.0824. Fractional part 0.0824. 10^0.0824≈1.21. So, 2^80≈1.21*10^24, first digit 1. Actual value is 1,208,925,819,614,629,174,706,176, which starts with 1. 2^90: log10(2^90)=90*0.3010299957≈27.0927. Fractional part 0.0927. 10^0.0927≈1.238. Therefore, 2^90≈1.238*10^27, first digit 1. Actual value is 1,237,940,039,285,380,274,899,124,224, which starts with 1. 2^100: as before, first digit 1. So, these examples confirm that the method works. Hence, for 2^400, following the same method, the first digit is 2. But just to be absolutely sure, let me check an online source or use a calculator if possible. Since I can't access external resources, I need to rely on my calculations. Alternatively, think of the following: if 2^400 starts with 2.58..., then doubling it 400 times would start with 2.58... times 10^120. But given that 2^10 is 1024, each multiplication by 2^10 increases the number by a factor of 1024, which is slightly more than 1000. So, over 40 multiplications by 1024, the leading coefficient increases by (1.024)^40≈2.585, which we calculated. Therefore, all methods point to the first digit being 2. Therefore, I conclude that the first digit of 2^400 is 2.Final Answerboxed{2}</think>To find the first digit of (2^{400}), we use the properties of logarithms. 1. Calculate (log_{10}(2^{400})): [ log_{10}(2^{400}) = 400 cdot log_{10}(2) ] Using the approximation (log_{10}(2) approx 0.3010299957): [ 400 cdot 0.3010299957 approx 120.41199828 ] This value has an integer part 120 and a fractional part 0.41199828.2. Use the fractional part to find the leading digits: [ 10^{0.41199828} approx 2.582 ] This means (2^{400}) is approximately (2.582 times 10^{120}).3. The leading digit of (2.582 times 10^{120}) is 2.Thus, the first digit of (2^{400}) is (boxed{2}).]

🔑:25.35. Answer: 2. The first digit of the number 2^{400}=left(2^{10}right)^{40}=1024^{40} coincides with the first digit of the number 1,024^{40}. On the one hand, according to problem 25.34, we get 1,024^{40}1+40 cdot 0,024+frac{40 cdot 39}{2} 0,024^{2}=2,40928>2.
